ー

第27回東北学生ラクロスリーグ戦の開幕式の試合のライブ配信をラクプラが東北大学と岩手大学と合同で行うこととなりました。
配信はラクプラが、コンテンツは各チームからという新し形でのコラボレーションをして配信を行います。

ライブ視聴中の選手やチームへのたくさんの温かい応援コメント、お待ちしております。

ー スケジュール
13：00 ~ ライブ配信開始
13：50 ~ スターティングメンバ―紹介
13：30 ~ 試合開始（試合時間は約2時間を予定しています）

ー 実況解説担当
佐藤 飛夕宇（東北大学OB）
川合 将矢（東北大学OB）
佐々木隼世（岩手大学OB）
津田 悠介（岩手大学OB）

ー 配信サポート
仙台ラクロスクラブ（SLC）

ー ライブ配信URL
https://youtu.be/rm2ah9Y7Yto"

2022/01/25(火)
14:26	飯田遥斗	"Oxidation State of the Atoms in an Organic Molecule

When dealing with transformation reactions, it is important to know whether elec-trons have been transferred between the reactants. For evalnating the number of electrons transfemed, it is convenient to examine the (formal) oxidation states of all
atoms involved in the reaction. Of particular interest to us will be the oxidation state of carbon, nitrogen, and sulfur in a given organic molecule, since these are the elements most frequcutly involved in organic redox reactions.

The terms oxidation and reduction refer, respectively, to the loss and gain of elec-
trons at an atots or ion. An oxidation state of zero is assigned to the uncharged
element; a loss of Z electrons is then an oxidation to an oxidation state of + Z.
Similarly, a gain of electrons leads to an oxidation state lower by an ambount equal tothe number of gained electrons. A simple example is the oxidation of sodium by chlorine, resulting in the formation of sodium chloride:

To bridge the gap between full electron transfer in ionic redox reactions (as shown in bonds, one can formally assign the possession of the clectron pair in a covalent bond the example above) and the situation with the shared electrons encountered in covalent to the more electronegative atom of the two bonded atoms. By doing so, one can then
count the electrons on each atom as one would with simple inorganic ions. For any
atom in an organic molecule, the oxidation state may be computed by adding O for
each bond to an identical atom; - I for each bond to a less electronegative atom or for each negative charge on the atom; and + I for each bond to a more electronegative atom or for each positive charge. We note that in C-S, C-I, and even C-P bonds the electrons are attributed to the heteroatom, although the clectonegativities of these heteroatoms are very similar to that of carbon. Finally, we should also point out that Roman instead of Arabic numbers are frequently used to express the oxidation state of a covalently bound atom.

Some examples demonstrating how to deternine the oxidation states of the carbon atoms present in organic molecules are given in Illustrative Example 2.1. More examples covering the other elements that may assume several different oxidation states (i.e., N, S, P) will be given in Section 2.3 in our discussion of functional groups."
